Intro

1. Starting to think responsively

Building websites used to be simple. We had a single design, we made the site look exactly like that design, and we were done.

Now we must make a design and have it work on every device, from a watch screen to a 80â€³ TV.

Now that we have the fundamentals down, itâ€™s time to get into the responsive frame of mind.

Weâ€™ll work our way up from getting a design to simply work on a single screen size to being able to get it to work on any device.

Thinking responsively isnâ€™t easy, especially at first.

Weâ€™ll work our way up, exploring the doâ€™s and donâ€™ts and how to plan things out to make your life easier in the long run.

This is just an intro thinking responsively.

We wonâ€™t be covering everything, but this is to set the stage for everything that comes after it.

What weâ€™ll be covering in this module:

  • How to approach a layout
  • CSS units (absolute, relative, and percentages)
  • Flexbox basics (our layout tool)
  • Media query basics (adapting to different screen sizes)

2. CSS Units

CSS Units

Starting to think responsively

There are different types of units that we can use

CSS units come in a few different flavors:

  • Absolute units
  • Relative units
  • Percentage

CSS Units

Absolute units are the easiest to understand.

  • Pixels (px)
  • pt, cm, mm, in, etc

CSS Units â€“ Percentage

Percentages are mainly used for widths, and are pretty easy to understand.

  • Relative to their parent (except on height, where things get weird).

CSS Units â€“ Relative units

There are two types of relative units.

  • Relative to font-size
  • Relative to the viewport

Even though it sounds complicated, once you play around with them a little it isnâ€™t too bad!

CSS Units â€“ Relative units

  • Relative to font-size:
    • em and rem (and many other less common ones)
  • Relative to the viewport (weâ€™ll get to this later)
    • vw, vh, vmin, vmax

3. CSS Units â€“ Percentage

Percentages

  • å¯¬åº¦ â€“ 960px
  • å·¦é‚Š â€“ 960 * 0.7 = 672px
  • And then there is the other side
  • And what about the padding?
  • And thatâ€™s for a fixed width!
  • We need to think responsively

width: 620px; â†’ width: 50%;

  width: 50%;
  margin: 0 auto;

6. CSS Units â€“ The em unit

The â€œemâ€ unit

Starting to think responsively

CSS Units â€“ Relative units

Relative units are always relative either to a font-size, or the size of the viewport.

For those which are relative to a font-size, which font-size they are relative to depends on a few things, which complicates matters.

CSS Units â€“ Relative units

The em and rem are considered relative, because they are relative to the font-size of other elements

  • em are relative to their parentâ€™s font-size
  • Font-size is an inherited property, so if you donâ€™t declare it anywhere, itâ€™s getting it from the body (or the default, which is normally 16px)
  • Letâ€™s see how they work

8. The Solution: Rems

The solution: rems

Starting to think responsively

CSS Units â€“ The problem with em

The rem unit is short for Root Em.

That means itâ€™s always relative to the â€œrootâ€ of our document.

The root of an HTML page is always the html element.

ç¨‹å¼ç¢¼ç¯„ä¾‹

9. Picking which unit to use

How to decide which unit to use

Starting to think responsively

How to decide which unit to use

Pixels used to cause a lot of problems, as they were a fixed unit (one dot on the screen).

Now, it follows the reference pixel.

How to decide which unit to use

That also makes my answer a little less concrete than I use to have.

But there are advantages to using rem and em.

How to decide which unit to use

So Iâ€™m going to stick with those.

My general rule of thumb:

  • Font-size = rem
  • Padding and margin = rem
  • Widths = em or percentage
  • Those are a â€œrule of thumbâ€, not hard and fast rules
  • Letâ€™s see how they work

11. Flexbox refresher and setting up some HTML

Responsive layout basics

Starting to think responsively

The basics of flexbox

Elements normally have a display: block or a display: inline as a default from the browser.

æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹

CSS

.parent {
  display: flex;
}

The basics of flexbox

Block elements stack on the top of the each other

  • div, header, footer, main, etc
  • h1 â†’ h6
  • p

The basics of flexbox

Inline elements stay within the flow of whatâ€™s around them

  • a
  • strong
  • em
  • span

The basics of flexbox

We can change this behavior by setting the display property to flex on the parent element.

ç¶²ç«™çµæ§‹
  • .container
    • .columns
      • .col
      • .col
      • .col

      • .col
      • .col

17. Media Query basics

Media queries â€“ the basics

Starting to think responsively

  • Media queries let us add new styles that target only specific conditions

Media queries â€“ basic syntax

@media () { â€¦ }

Media queries â€“ basic syntax

@media media-type () { â€¦ }

Media queries â€“ basic syntax

@media media-type and (media-features) { â€¦ }

Media queries â€“ media type

The media type letâ€™s us target different types of media

  • Screen @media screen { â€¦ }
  • Print @media print { â€¦ }
  • Speech @media speech { â€¦ }

Media queries â€“ media condition

The media condition letâ€™s us target specific conditions within that media type

  • Widths @media (min-width: 600px) { â€¦ }
  • Orientation @media (orientation: landscape) { â€¦ }
  • Specific features @media (hover) { â€¦ }

Media queries

Both media types and conditions are optional.

We do need to either have a type or condition though.

Media queries

For example, we can target only screens.

@media screen { â€¦ }

Media queries

Or we can choose only a condition, such as the width of the viewport.

@media (min-width: 960px) { â€¦ }

Media queries

You can combine a type with a condition by using and.

@media screen and (min-width: 960px) { â€¦ }

@media screen and (orientation: portrait) { â€¦ }

  • For now, weâ€™re focused onÂ min-widthÂ andÂ max-width
  • Letâ€™s start with a simple example

18. Making out layout responsive with flex-direction

Making our layout fully responsive

Starting to think responsively

Flexbox â€“ changing the axis around

æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹
display: flex /* creates columns */
flex-direction: row; /* default behavior */

Flexbox â€“ changing the axis around

display: flex /* creates columns */
flex-direction: column; /* switches the axis */
.parent {
  display: flex;
  flex-direction: column;
}

19. flex-direction explained

Flex-direction explained

Starting to think responsively

Flexbox â€“ changing the axis around

display: flex; /* creates columns */
flex-direction: row; /* default behavior */

Flexbox â€“ changing the axis around

display: flex; /* creates columns */
flex-direction: column; /* switches the axis */
æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹
.parent {
  display: flex;
  flex-direction: column;
}
  • We are switching the main axis
æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹
.parent {
  display: flex;
  justify-content: space-between;
}
æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹
.parent {
  display: flex;
  justify-content: space-between;
  flex-direction: column;
}
  • justify-content will now work vertically
    align-items will now work horizontally
æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹
.parent {
  display: flex;
  align-items: flex-start;
}
.parent {
  display: flex;
  align-items: flex-start;
  flex-direction: column;
}
æŠ•å½±ç‰‡ç¯„ä¾‹
.parent {
  display: flex;
  align-items: center;
}
.parent {
  display: flex;
  align-items: center;
  flex-direction: column;
}
.parent {
  display: flex;
  flex-direction: column;
  align-items: center;
  justify-content: space-between;
}

20. Creating a navigation

Creating a navigation

Starting to think responsively

Creating a navigation

All this flex stuff really comes into play when styling a responsive navigation for a site.

Creating a navigation

Navigations are traditionally marked up using an unordered list, which is placed inside a <nav> element.

Creating a navigation

<nav>
  <ul>
    <li><a href="index.html">home</a></li>
    <li><a href="about.html">about me</a></li>
    <li><a href="recent-posts.html">recent posts</a></li>
  <ul>
</nav>
Colors
  • #f8f8f8
  • #707070
  • #1792d2
  • #143774
font-sizes
  • Title: Lora â€“ 3.375rem
  • Subtitle: Ubuntu bold â€“ 1rem
  • Navigation: Ubuntu bold â€“ 1rem
ç¨‹å¼ç¢¼ç¯„ä¾‹

  <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0">
  <title>intro to rwd</title>
  <link href="https://fonts.googleapis.com/css2?family=Lora&family=Ubuntu:wght@700&display=swap" rel="stylesheet">
  <link rel="stylesheet" href="css/all.css">
</head>
<body>

  <header>
    <div class="site-title">
      <h1>ç¨‹åºå“¡é‚£éº¼å¯æ„›</h1>
      <p class="subtitle">Cute Programmer</p>
    </div>

      <ul>
        <li><a href="#">é¦–é </a></li>

  padding: 2em 0;

/* navigation */

nav ul {
  list-style: none;
  padding: 0;
  display: flex;
  justify-content: center;
}

nav li {
  margin-left: 2em;
}

nav a {
  text-decoration: none;
  color: #707070;
  font-weight: 700;
  padding: 0.25em 0;
}

nav a:hover,
nav a:focus {
  color: #1792d2;
}

.current-page {
  border-bottom: 1px solid #707070;
}

.current-page:hover {
  color: #707070;
}

.current-page:hover {
  color: #707070;
}

@media (max-width: 675px) {
  nav ul {
    flex-direction: column;
  }

  nav li {
    margin: 0.5em 0;
  }
}

/* articles */

.article-featured {
  border-bottom: 1px solid #707070;
  padding-bottom: 2em;
  margin-bottom: 2em;
}

.article-recent {
  display: flex;
  flex-direction: column;
  margin-bottom: 2em;
}

.article-recent-main {
  order: 2;
}

.article-recent-secondary {
  order: 1;
}

@media (min-width: 550px) {
  .article-recent-main {
    margin-top: -2.5em;
  }

  .article-info {
    text-align: right;
  }
}

  margin: 0;
}

a {
  color: #1792d2;
}

a:hover,
a:focus {
  color: #143774;
}

strong {
  font-weight: 700;
}

/* h1 subtitle */
.subtitle {
  font-weight: 700;
  color: #1792d2;
  font-size: 0.75rem;
  margin: 0;
}

.article-title {
  font-size: 1.5rem;
}

.article-read-more,
.article-info {
  font-size: 0.875rem;
}

.article-read-more {
  color: #1792d2;
  text-decoration: none;
  font-weight: 700;
}

.article-read-more:hover,
.article-read-more:focus {
  color: #143774;
  text-decoration: underline;
}

.article-info {
  margin: 2em 0;
}


/* =================
   Layout
   ================= */

.container {
  width: 90%;
  max-width: 900px;
  margin: 0 auto;
}

.container-flex {
  display: flex;
  flex-direction: column;
  justify-content: space-between;
}

header {
  background: #f8f8f8;
  padding: 2em 0;
  text-align: center;
  margin-bottom: 3em;
}

@media (min-width: 675px) {
  .container-flex {
    flex-direction: row;
  }

  main {
    width: 75%;
  }

  aside {
    width: 20%;
  }
}

/* navigation */

nav ul {
  list-style: none;
  padding: 0;
  display: flex;
  justify-content: center;
}

nav li {
  margin-left: 2em;
}

nav a {
  text-decoration: none;
  color: #707070;
  font-weight: 700;
  padding: 0.25em 0;
}

nav a:hover,
nav a:focus {
  color: #1792d2;
}

.current-page {
  border-bottom: 1px solid #707070;
}

.current-page:hover {
  color: #707070;
}

@media (max-width: 675px) {
  nav ul {
    flex-direction: column;
  }

  nav li {
    margin: 0.5em 0;
  }
}

/* articles */

.article-featured {
  border-bottom: 1px solid #707070;
  padding-bottom: 2em;
  margin-bottom: 2em;
}

.article-recent {
  display: flex;
  flex-direction: column;
  margin-bottom: 2em;
}

.article-recent-main {
  order: 2;
}

.article-recent-secondary {
  order: 1;
}

  .article-recent {
    flex-direction: row;
    justify-content: space-between;
  }

  .article-recent-main {
    width: 68%;
  }

  .article-recent-secondary {
    width: 30%;
  }

  .article-featured {
    display: flex;
    flex-direction: column;
  }

  .article-image {
    order: -2;
  }
  .article-info {
    order: -1;
  }
}

44. Important Note. The viewport meta tag

The viewport meta

Starting to think responsively

The viewport meta tag

One thing that I donâ€™t include the lessons in this module is the viewport meta tag.

When we take a browser window on our desktop and play with the width, it works well, but it doesnâ€™t fully simulate how a phone will render a page.

The viewport meta tag

To prevent problems form rendering pages that are not optimized for mobile devices and small screens, phones and tablets will actually render a zoomed out version of the page and then shrink it down to fit their viewport.

The viewport meta tag

To prevent it from doing this on our pages, which weâ€™ve optimized for small screens with media queries, we need to add a meta tag to the head of our document.

The viewport meta tag

There are a lot of different attributes that we can add, but the basic meta that youâ€™ll use looks like this:

<meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1">

It can be added anywhere in the <head> of your document.

The viewport meta tag

This will ensure that your page actually loads properly on a mobile device, which after all the hard work we did, we really want to do!

So once youâ€™ve graduated from Scrimba and youâ€™re working on your own pages and testing them in different devices, remember to include this in your <head>!

ç¨‹å¼ç¢¼ç¯„ä¾‹

HTML

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html lang="en">
<head>
  <meta charset="UTF-8">
  <meta http-equiv="X-UA-Compatible" content="IE=edge">
  <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0">
  <title>The viewport meta tag</title>
</head>
<body>
  
</body>
</html>
